Now consider financing the property’s acquisition. The terms you agreed upon with the lender for a
fee simple purchase are completely out of whack for the purchase of the property subject to the ground lease.
If you attempt to borrow $65 million to purchase the building, subject to the $3 million annual ground lease,
it is a 113.8% LTV ($65 million / $57.1 million), as the $42.9 million land value is not yours to give as
collateral. In addition, the interest coverage ratio for such a loan is only 1.42x ($5 million / $3.51 million), as
$3 million of the $8 million NOI goes towards ground lease payments. Of course, the lender will know about
the ground lease obligation and will use $5 million, not $8 million, as the relevant NOI when calculating
interest coverage for your purchase subject to the ground lease. As a result, lenders will not lend you as much
to purchase a property subject to a ground lease as for fee simple ownership.
The good news is that since you are purchasing the property subject to a ground lease, instead of
purchasing fee simple, you only need about $57 million to purchase the building, rather than the $100 million
required for the purchase of the building and the land. If you purchase the building for $57 million subject to
the ground lease using a 65% LTV mortgage, you will receive a $37 million ($57 * 65%) loan. But the lender
will charge a higher interest rate or impose tougher covenants on this loan, due to the fact that the ground

147

CHAPTER 15

DR. PETER LI N NEMAN

lease payments increase the building’s NOI risk. Assume that the lender charges an interest rate which is 30
basis points higher (hence a 5.7% interest rate, 210 basis points above Treasury), for a $2.12 million annual
interest payment each year. With $5 million pro forma NOI (net of $3 million in ground lease payments), the
pro forma interest coverage ratio in this case is 2.36x. These key ratios are in line with the common lender
underwriting criteria.
The lender may demand that the landowner agree to subordinate the ground lease claim to the
mortgage. This subordination means that the landowner accepts a lower position in terms of rights to the
collateral and the cash flows throughout the life of the loan. Subordinating the land means that if you default
on your mortgage, the lender not only has recourse to your building, but also to the landowner’s land, to
satisfy their mortgage claim. This underscores that the details of a loan contract are everything. Even though
the interest coverage ratio mathematically remains at 1.42x, such contractual terms with the lender make the
coverage ratio effectively much higher.
Such a mortgage proposal might be acceptable to both the borrower and the lender. But what about
the poor landowner? You want the landowner to agree to receive his ground lease payment after the lender,
and in the event that you default on your mortgage, the landowner could lose the land to the lender. What do
you think the landowner is going to say to this proposal? “No way!” You begin to see the problems involved
with separate owners of the land and the building. You can only give one absolute first lien, but both the
lender and the landowner want that position. When you split ownership of the land and the building, it is
hard to make everyone happy. That is why the ground lease structure is generally rare and usually inefficient.
CLOSING THOUGHT
Although ground leases are not involved in most real estate transactions, understanding the
complexities they create makes you a more informed analyst. Further, be sure to evaluate carefully the details
of the ground lease before investing in a situation where they are involved.

148

DR. PETE R LINNEMAN

CHAPTER 16

Chapter 16
Real Estate Exit Strategies
One of the more interesting changes in the real estate industry since the early 1990s is that people
now spend considerable time thinking about their exit strategies prior to acquiring or developing a property.
It used to be that people would all too frequently invest in real estate without considering when and how they
would exit. Investors today try to avoid the “roach motel” phenomenon: you can get in, but you can’t get
out. And getting out is important, because that is how you free up your equity and move on to better things.
WHY EXIT?
Why might you decide to exit? After all, you spent a lot of time assessing whether you wanted to
invest, and even more time working the property after the purchase. But if you are a developer, for example,
you are in the business of creating buildings, and may need equity for new developments. So developers need
an exit strategy before investing, so that they can free up their capital for future developments. Once the
property is stabilized and generating a solid return, a developer may need to exit in order to build again.
Another entity, such as a REIT or local operator, may have a comparative advantage in managing stabilized
properties.
Another reason to exit is to alter your risk-reward profile. For example, a developer specializes in
taking development risks. However, once the building is stabilized, this risk is gone. Similarly, an older
building in need of repositioning has more risk than a new building. If you are unwilling or unable to deal
with the complexities, costs, and challenges posed by an older building, you will want to exit. If you are
purchasing a new strip center because it is the best in the market, as demographics, the local economy,
consumer preferences, competitive centers, and tenants all change, at some point you will need to exit, as the
building will no longer satisfy your investment criteria.
Another example is that real estate private equity funds generally exit once the “opportunity” has
been realized, in order to generate higher IRRs. In addition, these funds must have an exit strategy, as they
have finite lives of 7-12 years.
There are also “old-fashioned” reasons to exit. For example, people often exit inherited estate
properties, as the beneficiaries rarely want the headaches of managing real estate. In addition, situations such
as divorces and partnership dissolutions frequently force people to exit their investments. These human
aspects of investing force you to have an exit strategy.
HOW TO EXIT
What is the best exit strategy? To illustrate the pros and cons of several alternative exit strategies,
assume that three years ago, you bought a poorly managed property, Jessica Crest, for $100 million, with the
expectation of substantially enhancing property value through active management and re-leasing. You
purchased the building with $40 million of your equity and a $60 million mortgage. For IRS (depreciation)
purposes, you allocate $75 million of the purchase price to structure, and $25 million to land (which is not
depreciable).

149

CHAPTER 16

DR. PETER LIN NEMAN
FIGURE 16.1

After three years of hard work, you have successfully repositioned the property, and its value has indeed risen
to the expected $140 million, increasing your equity from $40 million to $80 million ($140 million - $60
million of debt).
You have successfully executed your business plan, but now you have a problem. Having stabilized
the property, you believe it highly unlikely that you can double your equity via this property over the next
three years. You have identified another mismanaged property and want to repeat the repositioning strategy.
Purchasing the identified property requires you to come up with $40 million in equity, but you do not have
$40 million available, as all of your money is tied up in Jessica Crest. How do you access this built-up equity
in Jessica Crest?
FIGURE 16.2

SALE
One option is an outright sale of Jessica Crest. If you decide to sell Jessica Crest, you have to go
through the process of finding a buyer. Depending on the building and market, a “typical” sales process
requires 3-12 months. To prepare the property for sale, you must gather your documents and contracts,
prepare financial statements, have an accounting firm audit those statements, perhaps create a sales book, hire
lawyers to negotiate and structure the sale agreement, possibly hire a broker to help sell the property, spend
time negotiating with buyers, and tend to other concerns, as well. In addition to the time and energy
involved, the sales process is not free, as lawyers, accountants, and brokers do not work for free. Many of the
costs of these services are relatively fixed. As a result, fees as a percentage of the total sale price tend to be
larger for smaller properties, and smaller for larger properties. In general, 2%-5% is a good estimate for the
costs of selling a property, but keep in mind that the actual percentage fluctuates with property value. If you
have a large trophy asset, like Rockefeller Center, the fees as a percentage of value will be substantially less,
because the amount of work and value added by the brokers are not large relative to the value of the
property. If, however, you have a $5 million property, then the fees can easily exceed 5%. Assume that
selling Jessica Crest will cost you $3 million in fees.
Further assume that after six months of searching for a buyer, you successfully sell Jessica Crest for
$140 million. You will use the $140 million sale proceeds to repay the $60 million mortgage. If the buyer
uses debt financing to purchase the building, there are technical complications in the final closing, as the
buyer will be unable to pay you before receiving a mortgage. However, the buyer’s lender will not provide a
mortgage without a first lien on the property, which cannot be given until you sell the property and repay
your loan. To solve this problem, you will have a simultaneous closing on the sale, repayment of the existing
loan, and transfer of the first lien to the purchaser’s lender.

150

D R . P ETE R LI N N E MAN

CHAPTER 16

Another major payment you have to make upon sale is your capital gains tax. Under U.S. tax code,
there are two components of the capital gains tax. The first component is the actual gain you achieve on the
building. In this case, you paid $100 million for the property and you sold it for $140 million. This $40
million gain is (currently) taxed at 15%, for a total of $6 million in taxes. But remember that the capital gains
tax rate changes over time as tax law evolves.
The second capital gains tax component is that you have to pay taxes on the property’s accumulated
depreciation. You have been depreciating the building over the past three years to shield income from
ordinary income taxes. The government (currently) requires that you pay a 25% tax rate on accumulated
depreciation. Assuming you depreciated the $75 million you originally attributed to structure over a 40-year
period ($1,875 million per year), you would have taken $5,625 million in total accumulated deprecation over
the past three years (($75 million / 40)*3). In reality, accumulated depreciation will be larger, as not all value
will be attributable to structure, as opposed to carpet, fixtures, etc. The government will tax the $5,625
million accumulated depreciation at a 25% tax rate, or $1.4 million in taxes. If you are a non-taxable entity,
such as a pension fund, the capital gains tax is zero. Combining these capital gains tax components, you will
pay a total of $7.4 million in capital gains taxes upon the $140 million sale.
FIGURE 16.3

Thus, after repaying the loan, paying all fees and paying the IRS, you are left with $67.6 million of the $140
million in gross sales proceeds.
FIGURE 16.4

151

CHAPTER 16

D R . P E T E R I, 1 N N E M A N

In addition, your lender may require you to pay a pre-payment penalty or a yield maintenance fee for
repaying your loan prematurely. This analysis assumes that you have no pre-payment penalty on the loan. In
our example, you will probably not incur a prepayment penalty, because given your repositioning strategy, you
would have selected a loan that allowed you to sell the building after roughly 3-4 years without penalty. You
probably used a floating rate loan with a relatively short maturity, in order to avoid a prepayment penalty
upon exit. This exemplifies how the type of debt selected depends on your investment and exit strategy.
Upon completion of the sale process, you have sufficient funds to execute the other repositioning
deal, as you have $69.6 million, and only needed $40 million in equity for the new project. However, freeing
up your money came at a cost. Specifically, even though you doubled your equity from $40 million to $80
million on the investment, you only have $69.6 million in your pocket, due to the substantial leakage from
fees and taxes. Are there exit alternatives available which reduce these leakages from sale?
x.

REFINANCING
An alternative exit strategy for the Jessica Crest property is refinancing - that is, taking out a new loan
on the property. If the original lender was willing to lend $60 million on the $100 million Jessica Crest
property prior to repositioning, a lender should be willing to lend you substantially more on a stabilized
Jessica Crest worth $140 million. For example, if you obtain a new 65% LTV, or $91 million, the first
payment must once again go to the current lender to repay the original mortgage (see Figure 16.2), leaving
you with $31 million in refinancing proceeds ($91 million - $60 million to the existing lender).
The refinancing process is not free. Although it is much cheaper than the sales process, as you do
not have to pay the transfer taxes associated with selling the property, you will probably have a 50-basis-point
loan fee, as well as some lawyers’ and accounting fees. But even if you pay $1 million in refinancing costs, it
is a notable improvement over the costs associated with the sale option. More importantly, if you refinance,
you defer paying capital gains taxes, as you only pay capital gain taxes upon sale. This is the major advantage
of the refinance option relative to a sale. Of course, you must eventually pay taxes when you sell the building
someday, but by refinancing, you are able to defer these taxes, and given the time value of money, you prefer
to defer this tax liability. In fact, the longer you think you will hold the building, the more attractive the
refinance option. For example, if you plan to sell the building tomorrow, the refinancing option makes no
sense, because you simply pay $1 million in refinancing fees, as well as the sale-related fees and taxes. But if
you plan to hold the property for 60 years, the present value of the deferred capital gains tax liability is
effectively zero.
FIGURE 16.5

Upon refinancing Jessica Crest, you realize net refinancing proceeds of $30 million. Thus, although
you avoid $2 million in fees and $7.4 million in gains taxes, the net proceeds from refinancing the property
are $39.6 million lower than from selling the building ($69.6 million from sale versus $30 million from

152

DR. P E T E R L I N N E M A N

CHAPTER 16

refinancing). Where did the remaining money go? The answer is that you still own a $49 million equity
position in Jessica Crest in the case of refinancing, as the building is worth $140 million against a $91 million
loan. Therefore, your total wealth position after refinancing is $79 million ($30 million in cash from the
refinancing plus $49 million in equity in the property), which is higher than the $69.6 million from selling the
building.
Yet after refinancing, you still have a problem, as you need $40 million in cash for the new
repositioning deal. For that purpose, refinancing does not satisfy your objective. Of course, if the increase in
value were greater, or the refinancing LTV were higher, the refinancing option could achieve this goal. Or if
you only need $30 million for your next deal, refinancing is very effective. But in our example, too much
value is left tied up in Jessica Crest to achieve your objective via refinancing.
What can you do to solve this equity gap? You could take on a smaller repositioning project, or sell a
slice of either Jessica Crest or the new project to an equity partner. One problem with finding an equity
partner is that there are relatively few buyers of minority interests in properties, as relatively few investors
want an illiquid ownership stake, particularly where their vote will never impact decisions.
Of course, investing $40 million in another property is not every investor’s objective, and thus the
refinancing option is very attractive for investors who are happy to remain invested in the stabilized property.
For those happy to take $30 million out of Jessica Crest and keep the $49 million equity position, the
refinancing option is perfect, because of the lesser value leakage relative to sale. Remember that there is no
single objective for all investors. Numbers are important, but who you are and what you are trying to
accomplish ultimately dictates your exit strategy.
LIKE KIND EXCHANGE (1031 EXCHANGE)
The third exit possibility is U.S.-specific. Under U.S. tax code (at least currently), you can pursue a
so-called like kind exchange, which essentially allows you to sell your property free of state and federal tax if
you purchase a “similar” property, hence forestalling capital gains taxes until you ultimately break the
ownership chain. Also, the 1031 exchange allows you to restart your depreciation schedule. This is
particularly helpful if you have a building that is nearing the end of its depreciable life. Under the like kind
exchange principle, you can exchange your partnership interest for a partnership interest in a different
partnership. The IRS ultimately decides which changes are considered “like kind”. The exchange is not
taxable until you sell your interest for a different type of asset, shares, or cash.
In real estate, you can sometimes utilize the like kind exchange strategy to defer tax payments
otherwise due upon sale. In our Jessica Crest example, you would sell the $140 million Jessica Crest building
exactly as before. You will pay the sale-related fees and retire the debt, but you do not pay the capital gains
taxes immediately. You then have roughly three months to identify another property for purchase which the
IRS will deem a qualified like kind property (this can be a highly technical tax determination). If you find a
building that qualifies under the tax code, you can buy the building and treat the entire transaction as an
exchange, even though you actually sold the old property and bought the new property rather than
exchanging properties. Volumes of tax law dictate the circumstances under which you can effectuate a like
kind exchange and defer taxes, and the nuances change constantly.
There are some general guidelines you can use to understand the application of like kind exchange as
an exit strategy, but you must make certain that the new property is being held for investment or productive
use. The property cannot be acquired with the intent of being resold after a short time period. One of the
criteria is that the items exchanged must generally be the same business. For example, you cannot sell Jessica
Crest and buy an oil and gas company, stocks, or bonds, and treat it as a like kind exchange. Broadly
speaking, whether a property is considered like kind is up to the “nature and character of the property, not its

153

CHAPTER 16

D R . P ETE R LI N N EMA N

grade or quality” 1031(a)-l(b). This is an imprecise definition. You may do exchanges on both improved and
unimproved property. Also, city real estate and farm real estate may be considered like kind exchanges.
Obviously, land in New York City is very different from farmland in Chester, Virginia, but the IRS generally
views these as being different only in quality or grade. The IRS does not consider real estate outside the
United States as like kind property. Real estate in India and Japan, however, may be considered like
properties, as foreign real properties are like kind for other foreign properties.
In sum, you have to sell your real estate and buy another “like” property. But what is a “like”
property? You can probably do different property types, in different cities, in different submarkets. 1031
exchanges are underused and often misunderstood. Rural landowners sometimes use 1031 exchanges as a
way to enter urban markets. Basically, land not producing income is sold for income producing space in
urban locations without the burden of capital gains taxes. On the other hand, you probably cannot sell a fully
leased office building and buy an empty office building. Properties that both have less than 30-year leases
may be considered like kind. A vacant building does not have leases, while a fully occupied building may
have leases that extend past 30 years, making it most likely not a like kind situation, according the IRS.
There are two noteworthy margins on which people try to capitalize via like kind real estate
exchanges. The first involves selling a stabilized building while purchasing a building where there is
opportunity to add value. In our example, you would look to sell your stabilized Jessica Crest property for
the poorly managed, partially leased building you wanted to reposition. As long as you can get the IRS’
blessing of the repositioning purchase as a like kind exchange, you have satisfied your strategic objective of
purchasing the new value-added project while deferring your capital gains taxes on the sale of Jessica Crest.
The second opportunity is to sell the property and buy a stabilized building that offers a better
refinancing opportunity and little management burden. The poster child property in this case is one with a
long-term lease to the U.S. government. This is because lenders are willing to lend you more if secured
against a building with the U.S. government on a long-term lease than on Jessica Crest, due to the greater
tenant credit. As a result, you can net more proceeds out of the sale of Jessica Crest. Figure 16.6 illustrates
such an exit strategy.

CHAPTER 16

As before, you sell Jessica Crest for $140 million, repay the $60 million loan, and pay $3 million in
fees. But you will not pay capital gains tax, as long as the “replacement” purchase of the government leased
building qualifies as a like kind exchange. You buy the government leased building for $140 million, using a
higher 75% LTV mortgage due to the credit quality of the U.S. government lease. You use $35 million of
equity from the sale of Jessica Crest and the $105 million loan (75% * $140 million) to purchase the $140
million government building. You will also incur the $1 million in fees to borrow the $105 million, allowing
you to take out $41 million while retaining a $35 million equity position in the government leased property that is, a total equity position of $76 million. Although the $76 million derived from the like kind exchange is
less than the $79 million net value from a refinancing (due to the fees associated with selling Jessica Crest),
you are able achieve your objective, as the $41 million pulled out of the property provides you the $40 million
necessary to purchase and reposition another property (and put $1 million in your pocket). You are also able
to focus your energy on the repositioning exercise because the government leased property you own is a
relatively low headache management task. In contrast, with a straight refinancing, you still have to manage
the Jessica Crest property.
There are several structures to a successful 1031 transaction. Generally, you appoint an independent
specialist, known as a QI, or qualified intermediary, to help sell the property. The qualified intermediary’s
purpose is to set up an agreement so that an escrow or closing agent transfers the property to a buyer, so that
the sales proceeds go directly to the qualified intermediary. If this were not so, the 1031 exchanger would be
taxed on the proceeds. The exchanger must acquire a new property within a prescribed number of days. The
property must be designated as a “replacement” by the exchanger, and sent to the person from whom the
exchanger will acquire the property. This is known as the identification process. The exchanger must identify
a property that is not owned by a subsidiary or employee of the exchanger. The IRS Code includes a detailed
description of the people from whom the property cannot be purchased, known as disqualified persons.
About 180 days after the exchanger’s sale of the relinquished property, a like kind replacement property must
be purchased. Again, the purchase and sale contract is organized through the qualified intermediary, who
exchanges the proceeds to the seller, and transfers the property to the exchanger through a transfer of the
seller’s deed. This transaction has many intricacies, and a consultant is generally used. Note that it is nearly
impossible to find a property worth exactly the same as yours. For this reason, provisions are made that the
excess gain is taxed, with this excess known as the “Boot”. There are many types of like kind exchanges,
including three party exchanges, forward deferred exchanges, and reverse deferred exchanges. However,
these are all merely technical variants of that which is described above.
PUBLIC COMPANY
Another possible exit strategy is to exchange your ownership interest in Jessica Crest for a monetarily
equivalent ownership interest in a publicly traded company. For instance, you could exchange the $140
million Jessica Crest property for a 1.32% interest in the publicly traded stock of EOP, a $10.6 billion equity
market capitalization company. If this transaction is structured properly, you will again be able to defer taxes
and will not recognize capital gains on the transaction until you sell your EOP stock. Your tax basis from
Jessica Crest will carry over to the EOP stock, and your capital gains will be determined based on the
proceeds from the sale of the stock and the old basis. However, to achieve such deferral of capital gains will
require rather complex structuring. This is not a like kind exchange, but rather a minority interest.
Generally, you will receive dividend rights and voting rights equal to your pro rata share of the
company in an exchange with EOP. This transaction also allows you to access a larger, more diversified pool
of assets. In the case of EOP, you would now own 1.32% of 774 office properties throughout the U.S.
Furthermore, these assets may be better managed than you could achieve on Jessica Crest. In exchange for

155

CHAPTER

Z> R . PE T E R LI N N E M A N

16

these potential benefits, you will have to give up control of the property, and generally lose the property
management fees you earned. This is a frightening or positive consequence of this exit strategy, depending
upon your objectives.

GOING PUBLIC
The alternative to selling to a public company is to go public. In order to do so, you would need a
larger pool of assets than just Jessica Crest. Your company must consist of a large pool of diversified
properties in a particular property type, or the investor community will be unwilling to purchase your stock.
Furthermore, the pool of assets had better be structured in the manner the public market wants - low debt,
predictable cash flows, and reporting transparency. If your portfolio lacks these qualities, it is unlikely that
you will be able to receive fair value from the public market investor community. <
In addition, an IPO takes 12-18 months. If your IPO succeeds, you will incur total fees equal to 10%
of the total money raised. In fact, you will incur $500,000-$ 1 million in fees whether you complete the IPO
process or not. If you pursue an IPO, you are subject to the vagaries of timing and the market during the
process. If the market weakens, you may be unwilling to issue equity at substantially reduced prices. On the
other hand, if the IPO is successful, you will be able to access public capital markets, and can use the
proceeds to purchase additional properties or pay down debt.

CLOSING THOUGHT

yfT

J

h

!

Determining an exit strategy is critical. The optimal strategy ultimately depends on your objectives,
While the numbers and calculations are important, exit is ultimately a personal decision.

Two people looking

at the same numbers on the sale option and the refinance option may make completely different decisions
based on their objectives. Exit strategy is definitely not a “one size fits all” exercise!

CHAPTER 17

Chapter 17
Real Estate Private Equity Funds
Real estate private equity funds have evolved to fill the need for equity in the real estate business.
These funds are among the largest owners of real estate in the country, holding an estimated $100 billion of
real estate assets in the United States, as well as substantial portfolios abroad. It is necessary to explore briefly
the evolution of these funds in order to understand their role.

EVOLUTION
«

Historically, real estate was purchased and developed with lots of debt and very small equity
syndicates. For example, if you needed equity to fund a development project, you would go to your brother,
your cousin, and your neighbor, and try to raise the necessary equity. This structure worked pretty well when
lots of debt was available for real estate projects. If you had access to almost 100% debt financing, which was
not uncommon, you could probably find the necessary equity, especially if you had an established track
record. For example, with 99% leverage, if you want to do a $50 million project, you only have to come up
with $500,000 in equity. While that is a lot of money, an established player should be able to raise $500,000,
or they should not be in the business.
In addition to the availability of debt financing, depreciation laws allowed property owners to
depreciate structures over a 15-year period. Combined with even shorter depreciation schedules for
improvements, you could probably write off the entire building for tax purposes in 7-10 years. Assuming that
80% of the building was non-land, and you were writing the building off in 10 years, you were able to write
off (for tax purposes) about 8% of the value of the property each year. This meant that the owner of an 8
cap property could completely shelter all income solely via depreciation. Combined with 90-100% debt, the
tax shields from interest payments and depreciation generated enormous tax losses and modest cash flows
each year. Until 1986, you could sell these tax losses for cash to people like doctors and dentists, who valued
the losses to shelter their professional income from taxes. You could probably get 30-50 cents for every
dollar of tax loss you sold. Therefore, you were often able to raise the necessary equity through small equity
syndicate sales of the tax losses.
In 1986, Congress extended depreciation schedules and prohibited the sale of tax losses, except for
low-income housing and historic preservation. The swish of the President’s signature on this tax bill
eliminated the primarv source of real estate equity. When in the early 1990s lenders finally realized that they
had been vastly mispricing their real estate loans, and began offering only 50-70% loan-to-value mortgages,
rather than 90-100% mortgages, it meant that large chunks of equity were required to own or develop real
estate.
In this new environment, you need a large amount of equity to own real estate. For example, a 35%
equity slice on a $50 million project requires $17.5 million. This is a lot more than $500,000, and you cannot
raise a dime of it from the sale of tax losses. Raising $17.5 million in equity is a much more difficult and
sophisticated exercise than raising $500,000. You also run the risk that while you are raising the required
equity for the deal, you may lose the property to a better capitalized competitor. For example, a high net
worth individual or institutional investor can simply write a check to fund the project. Not surprisingly, if
you have a multi-billion dollar balance sheet, you generally have a readily available equity source for your real
estate purchases. But what about everyone else?

157

CHAPTER 17

D R . P E T E R L1 N N E M A N

In this new equity environment, investors with equity certainty have a frequent advantage. This is
because although the investor putting together an equity syndicate might be willing to bid more for a
property, the seller may accept a lower bid from a well capitalized bidder to ensure a quick closing. If you can
achieve even a 2-3% lower purchase price on a building with an expected 11% annual return, you realize
almost one third of a year’s return up front, simply by being able to write a check quickly. This access to
equity advantage led to the creation of real estate private equity funds, which raise large pools of equity before
investing in order to ensure ready access to equity.

FUNDS TODAY
The real estate private equity fund business probably started with the first Zell-Merrill fund in 1988.
Today there are perhaps 1,000 professionals working in the business (excluding accountants), versus maybe
50 in 1992. From 1992 to 2000, funds grew from almost no assets to more than $100 billion in assets.
However, the business is achieving maturity, with funds now raising $12-$18 billion in equity commitments
annually, to invest over roughly three years.
These funds raise money from pension funds, foundations, university endowments, and high net
worth individuals. They receive specific investment commitments from these investors, drawing down the
promised commitment on an “as needed” basis. The minimum investment commitment is usually at least $1
million, meaning that relatively few individuals directly invest in these funds, though many have a stake via
their pension funds.
Investors invest in a limited partnership managed by the fund sponsor, who serves as the general
partner responsible for all operations. If investors fail to satisfy a call on their funding commitments, they
forfeit many rights and economics. The fund does not take all of the committed money up front, because it
would have to invest the excess funds in low yielding cash until it was needed for the higher return real estate
investments. This would only serve to drag down fund return performance. Although pension funds and
other institutional investors would rather the real estate opportunity fund put the committed money to work
quickly, these investors are generally better suited to handle the excess cash position than are the funds. In
addition, pension funds and other institutions have lower total expected portfolio returns than these funds.
A typical target return for real estate private equity funds is about a 20% IRR, and a 2x equity
multiple over the life of the investment. Lately, these return targets are creeping downward. These high
target returns were originally necessary to lure investors back into a business that no one trusted in the early
1990s.
The typical fund promises to liquidate all property holdings within 7-10 years, as investors like
knowing when they will get their money back. The problem with this liquidation structure is that while the
properties may be great investments, the market could be weak as the fund’s termination date nears. To
resolve this problem, sponsors and investors generally can agree to an extension period of up to three years,
pending a vote of the majority of the limited partners. Although the weak market conditions may persist
beyond three years, most investors prefer to take reduced returns and close out the fund.
The sponsors of the fund go to pension funds, university and other endowments, and high net worth
individuals, requesting funding commitments. The sponsor retains the right to raise less or more than their
investment commitment target. For example, their target may be a fund size of $1 billion, but they can raise
more or less. Assuming they raise exactly $1 billion in commitments, the fund strategy is to invest the $1
billion in equity commitments over the next 2-4 years. The fund will leverage this equity when making its real
estate investments. At a 2:1 leverage target, i.e., 67% LTV, the fund will have roughly $3 billion in investment
power over the 2-4 year investment period.

158

D R . P E T E R L INNE M A N

CHAPTER 17

Once the fund has invested most of its capital, it will attempt to raise a new fund. The investors in
this second fund invest in a separate limited partnership, and have no claims on the assets held in the
sponsor’s first fund. It takes roughly 12-18 months, and perhaps $1 million in out-of-pocket costs, from the
time you begin seriously planning a fund to the time you close funding commitments. There are covenants in
the fund’s partnership documents that permit the sponsor to raise a new fund only after roughly 85% of the
fund’s capital is invested. This is designed to assure that the attention of the sponsor remains on the current
fund. Of course, the sponsor wants to ensure a seamless transition to its next fund, as it does not want to be
“out of business” for 12-18 months while the new fund is raised.
The sponsor also invests its capital in the fund. The amount that institutional investors require the
sponsor to commit depends on many factors, including the capital base of the fund sponsor. For example,
investors expect Goldman Sachs to put much more into ks $2 billion fund than a young real estate
entrepreneur starting a $30 million fund. This difference reflects the investor’s desire to make sure the
sponsor’s loss is significant if the fund fails to perform. The upper end investment among sponsors is 2550% of total equity commitments, while the low end is 1-3%.
WHO ARE THEY?
There are three broad groups of funds today: those associated with investment banks; those
associated with investment houses; and dedicated real estate players.
Investment Banks: The players in this group of funds include Morgan Stanley (MSREF), Goldman Sachs
(Whitehall), Lehman Brothers, UBS, J.P. Morgan/Chase, and CSFB. The investment banks attempt to
exploit their access to investors (especially high net worth individuals), deal flow, and capital market expertise.
No one has better access to a network of high net worth individuals than investment banks, especially
Goldman Sachs. For example, the majority of Goldman Sachs’ funds’ money derives from high net worth
investors, which is staggering, given the fact that Whitehall raises roughly $2 billion every three years. How
did they get that rolodex? Investment banks have sales groups and asset management groups that have long
focused on providing investments to high net worth individuals. With these established networks, it is
relatively easy for investment banks to access this source of capital. Given that the investment banks’
competitive advantage is raising money, they have hired or partnered with real estate people to run thek
funds.
The other advantage of investment banks is access to deal flow. For example, investment banks are
frequently intermediaries for companies or governments looking to sell properties. Therefore, investment
bank-sponsored funds see a lot of deals. The so-called “Chinese Wall” between principal and agent is riddled
with holes, with the private placement memorandum of every investment bank-sponsored fund describing
proprietary deal flow deriving from the firm’s client relationships. Finally, these shops are experts in
corporate finance, and have the expertise necessary to add value by creatively financing and selling complex
deals.
Investment Houses: Players in this group of funds include sponsors such as Blackstone, Carlyle, Cerberus,
Apollo, Angelo Gordon, and Oak Tree. Funds at these investment houses are generally stand-alone vehicles
which are part of a family of funds, including LBO funds, venture capital funds, hedge funds, and distressed
debt funds. The advantage of having multiple funds under the umbrella of an investment company is that
you are raising money for all the funds from the same investor base. To the extent they have performed well
on your other funds, they have enhanced credibility with capital sources on your real estate fund, as they can

159

CHAPTER 17

DR.? E T E R LIN N E M A N

go to a pension fund and say, “Remember how well you did in my LBO fund? Why don’t you invest some of
the money you made there in my real estate fund?”. Credibility and reputation are critical in the fundraising
business, and brand recognition facilitates the process, as pension funds and endowments, the two largest
sources of investment capital, are not in the business of taking risks. Remember that the individuals running
investments for these institutional capital sources are generally not incentivized to take risks. If a pension
fund invests in an unknown fund and loses money, the employee who made the decision might get fired. But
a poor return or even a loss via a name brand fund is more easily forgiven.
Dedicated Real Estate Players: The players in this group of opportunity funds include Starwood,
Lonestar, Westbrook, AEW, Lubert-Adler, Walton Street, Colony, JW O’Conner, Crow Holdings, and JE
Robert. Historically, the people who head these funds have worked in real estate most of their lives, and are
also skilled at raising money. They tend to be relatively focused real estate investors whose comparative
advantage is property-level expertise.
RETURN WATERFALL
In their marketing material, sponsors lay out the cash waterfall. This cash waterfall details how fund
proceeds are split between the investors and the sponsor. Let’s take as an example an investor, Prushansky
Company Pension, and a fund sponsor, Bhavik Fund. The sponsors of the Bhavik Fund provide investors
(including their own money) with a preferred IRR return on all commitments drawn (the “pref’). This
preferred return is usually 7-11% annually. Assume that the preferred annual return is 9%. If the
investments do not generate sufficient cash flow to pay 9% on all the invested equity, all profits generated by
operating cash flow or sale go to “the money”, which is all money invested, regardless of who invested it,
proportionate to the amount they invested. If the investments generate more than the preferred rate of
return, the sponsor and “the money” split the excess profits. The fund sponsor’s share of this profit is
referred to as a “promote” or “carried interest”. As long as the 9% preferred return is achieved, profits are
generally split 80/20, with “the money” receiving 80% of the profits, and the sponsor receiving 20% of the
profits. Keep in mind that the sponsor is part of “the money”, so it receives its share of the 80% profit slice
pari passu to the amount it invested. This structure provides an incentive for the sponsor to generate returns
in excess of the preferred return, as the sponsor realizes substantial profits once the preferred return is paid
to investors.
The sponsor’s promote is structured in one of two ways. In the most common structure, the
sponsor and “the money” split profits 80/20, as long as “the money” receives at least the annual preferred
return. Alternatively, there is an 80/20 split only on profits in excess of the preferred return. Figure 17.1
illustrates the difference.
Assume a $100 million fund with a 9% annual preferred return, with the sponsor contributing $10
million to the fund. Assume for simplicity that the fund liquidates in one year, after generating $25 million in
total profits after all costs and repaying debt. This $25 million profit represents a 25% annual return, which
exceeds the 9% preferred return. In the case of the structure where only profits above the preferred return
are split, “the money” first receives $9 million, which is the 9% preferred return, while the remaining $16
million of profits is split 80/20, with 80% going to “the money” ($12,800,000) and 20% to the sponsor
($3,200,000). The Bhavik Fund and Prushanksy Company Pension (assumed to be the only other investor)
split the $12.8 million going to “the money” pro rata based on the amount of money they contributed to the
fund. In our example, Prushansky Company Pension receives 90% of both the $12.8 million of excess profits
and the $9 million in preferred return payments, while the Bhavik Funds receive 10% of both plus their 20%

160

DR. PETER LIN NEMAN

CHAPTER 17

split of excess profits. In total, in this example, the sponsor (who provided 10% of the equity) receives $5.38
million of the profits, while the Prushansky Company Pension receives $19.62 million of the profits. So
although the fund generates a 25% return, under this structure, Prushansky Pension realizes a 21.8% ($19.62
million / $90 million) return on their investment.
On the other hand, as is the case for most real estate private equity funds, if the entire $25 million
profit is split 80/20, so long as “the money’7 receives at least a 9% return, the profit split is very different.
Note that in this structure, with a 9% preferred return, total profits must be at least $11.25 million before the
sponsor can claim a full 20% split of the profits. This is because 80% of $11.25 million leaves “the money”
with the minimum 9% preferred return. With $25 million in total profits, this structure gives “the money”
$20 million of the profits, with $5 million of the profits going to the sponsor. In addition, the sponsor
receives its pro rata share of the $20 million profit split going to “the money” (10% *$20 million = $2
million). This yields $7 million in total profits for the sponsor. As you can see, which fund structure is
chosen drastically impacts the profit distribution. For example, this structure provides the sponsor with a
total of $7 million, and the Prushansky Company Pension receives $18 million. Thus, although the fund
achieves a 25% return, the investor realizes only a 20% return ($18 million / $90 million). Not surprisingly,
fund sponsors prefer the structure which splits all profits, rather than the structure which only splits profits in
excess of the preferred return.

It is instructive to see the results of a waterfall structure as the total profits increase. The example
below is based on a 4-year fund with an 80/20 split for all profits, as long as the equity investor receives a 9%
preferred return and a 50/50 “catch up” provision. This provision says that all profits in excess of the
preferred return are split 50/50 until the sponsor has received 20% of all profits, after which all profits are
split 80/20. This is a very typical promote structure for funds, as the catch up provision improves the return
investors receive if the fund performs only modestly better than the preferred return.

Figures 17.2, 17.3, and 17.4 illustrate the split proceeds realized by an investor who has committed
$10 million to a fund with these terms, where it takes four years to realize these profits. As you can see, the
sponsor only receives its promote if the total profits result in a return greater than 9% for the equity investor.
As the profits increase, the value of the promote increases, creating a powerful incentive for the sponsor to
generate higher returns.

In addition to their promote, fund sponsors also receive an annual management fee. This fee is
typically 1-1.5% of committed equity (whether the money is drawn or not). As money is returned to
investors via asset sales, the base for these fees declines.

CLOSING THOUGHT
Like every aspect of the real estate business, the real estate private equity fund business is highly
technical and nuanced. The key to success in such funds is their ability to formulate and execute strategies
that yield returns substantially in excess of those available via public real estate companies. As is the case for
all real estate investments, the private equity structure is only as good as the ability to execute.

164

DR.

P E T E R LINNE M A N

CHAPTER 18

Chapter 18
REITs and Liquid Real Estate
Although the real estate business has been around for a long time, unprecedented changes in
financing and organizational structures have taken place since 1990. These changes are primarily due to the
requirement of significant amounts of equity in a previously debt-financed industry. One vehicle that has
evolved to meet the need for equity is publicly traded real estate companies, called “liquid real estate”. As of
year-end 2002, publicly traded real estate companies controlled roughly $380 billion worth of U.S. real estate
assets.
The key differences between publicly traded real estate companies and the other major real estate
equity vehicle, real estate private equity funds, are: investments in private equity funds are much less liquid;
private equity funds seek higher equity returns; since there are no investment minimums for public real estate
companies, they provide an opportunity to invest in real estate for all investors; and investors in public real
estate own both the real estate assets and the management team.
HISTORY OF REITS
REITs (Real Estate Investment Trusts) were created by Congress in 1960, in order to make
investments in large scale, income producing real estate accessible to all investors. REITs were not a major
force, however, until the early 1990s, as banks, insurance companies, tax syndicates, and pension funds
provided the bulk of capital for real estate.
Until undisciplined bank lending led to the collapse of real estate markets in 1990, REITs were a
niche financial vehicle with a mixed history and their share of booms and busts. For example, from 19701974, bank sponsored REITs made speculative development loans that ended with the mortgage REIT bust
of 1974. However, until the early 1990s, abundantly available debt meant that the major owners of real estate
did not need to access large pools of equity in order to own or develop real estate. In addition, since they had
tax losses due to high debt and large depreciation allowances, the REIT structure’s single taxation feature
offered no advantage. Only when lending rationalized and tax breaks disappeared did many of the leading
owners of real estate turn to public equity markets.
When lenders began requiring 25-50% in equity, many leading owners went public, in order to access
efficiently the necessary equity for their portfolios. This resulted in greater real estate market liquidity and
transparency. The total market capitalization of REITs exploded, from $12 billion at the end of 1990, to
roughly $380 billion at the end of 2002, as properties shifted from private to public ownership.
There are three REIT categories: equity REITs; mortgage REITs; and hybrid REITs. Equity REITs,
the dominant format, own and operate income producing real estate, while mortgage REITs lend money
directly to real estate owners, or extend credit indirectly through the acquisition of loans or mortgage backed
securities. The revenue from mortgage REITs primarily derives from interest on the mortgages they own,
while equity REITs derive their profits from rental streams. Hybrid REITs pursue a bit of both strategies.
Until the early 1990s, real estate was largely debt financed. If you could buy $1 billion worth of real
estate for just $1 of equity, and finance the rest via non-recourse debt, wouldn’t you take that deal, almost
irrespective of the real estate? Of course! If the value of the portfolio rises by just 1%, you make a $10
million profit on a $1 investment. And if the value falls, you only stand to lose $1 (and perhaps a bit of your
reputation). When 90-110% LTV loans were the industry norm, owners did not have to be particularly good
at operating real estate. Instead, the critical skill was the ability to convince lenders to lend.

165

DR.

CHAPTER 18

PETER LIN NEMAN

Once this excessive debt financing disappeared, large pools of equity and the ability to operate
properties efficiently became critical ingredients for success. As a result, many real estate entrepreneurs began
to specialize. After all, if you have to put significant amounts of your own money into a deal, you want to
invest where you believe you have a core competency. In addition, equity sources have increasingly funneled
their capital to the best operators who share an alignment of interests.

REIT IPO BASICS
As the 1990s began, there were few notable public REITs. Today, they own in excess of $400 billion
in real estate, and are a virtual “Who’s Who” of the real estate business. The reason for this change is that
until the early 1990s, real estate was the bastard child of global capital markets, sustaining itself on tax
gimmicks and excessive debt. Foolish tax legislation and undisciplined lending by banks, S&Ls, and life
companies led to massive overbuilding in the 1980s. As the economy softened in the early 1990s, financial
regulators literally shut down real estate lending. This caused a virtual collapse in a real estate industry
comprised primarily of highly leveraged developers reliant on continued debt refinancing to repay their
maturing debt. But as mortgages matured in the early 1990s, no new loans were available to repay these
maturing obligations.
For many leading real estate operators, there were only two viable financial options: filing a Chapter
11 bankruptcy, or filing an S-ll Initial Public Offering (IPO) prospectus with the SEC. The strategy for
going public was to sell the majority equity claim to your assets, and use the IPO’s net cash proceeds to pay
off maturing debt. For those nimble enough to complete a successful IPO, an S-ll was far more attractive
than a Chapter 11. The volume of real estate public offerings was very rapid, with $19.5 billion raised in 117
IPOs from 1992-1996. Once delevered, these newly public real estate companies were able to purchase
additional real estate, particularly by using their shares as currency to purchase properties from distressed
owners unable to navigate their own IPOs.
To see how the IPO process worked, consider Moy Realty, owner of an apartment portfolio in 1993
worth $100 million at a 9 cap on NOI. However, this $100 million valuation was only theoretical. The actual
value of these properties was highly uncertain, as the absence of debt meant that there were very few viable
purchasers for the properties. Hence, a large margin of error existed in assessing the value of the portfolio.
This margin was often plus or (more dangerously) minus 10%. Even at the questionable $100 million
valuation, the once proud multimillionaire Ms. Moy faced a severe financing squeeze, as she had $98 million
in mortgages against these properties. To make matters (much) worse, she had $56 million in debt maturing
in the next 24 months, with personal guarantees on $30 million of this outstanding debt. And this $98
million debt burden was the same whether the properties were worth $100 million or only $90 million. What
could Ms. Moy do to repay this maturing debt and free herself from personal guarantees that could leave her
penniless?
If she declared Chapter 11 bankruptcy, the process would buy her perhaps 12-24 months, during
which things might improve. However, absent a major market turnaround, she would eventually lose the
properties securing the maturing mortgages, the management fee streams associated with these lost
properties, as well as her other assets, due to her personal recourse, not to mention the capital gains taxes
triggered by the foreclosures. Alternatively, she could attempt to sell some of her prized properties to
generate enough cash to pay off the maturing obligations. But the success of this strategy was highly
questionable in a market populated with few buyers and many distressed sellers. Further, upon sale, she
would lose the management fees on these assets, would have to pay capital gain taxes, and would still be
personally liable for debt repayment shortfalls.

166

DR. PETER LIN NEMAN

CHAPTER 18

When she attempted to raise equity by selling limited partnership interests in her properties to
pension funds, insurance companies, or high net worth individuals, she was told, “No thanks, we have our
own real estate woes”, or “If I didn’t invest in your real estate partnerships when things were supposed to be
good, why would I invest now?!”. These potential capital sources were also troubled by the failure of real
estate operators to provide a return of capital via sale, as these operators always wanted to keep the properties
in order to generate management fees, arguing that it was never the right time to sell. And no lender would
provide more than a 50% LTV loan. But at such an LTV, she clearly lacked sufficient capital to repay her
maturing debt. And since her wealth was primarily in her properties, she lacked the resources to repay the
loan gap with a personal equity infusion. These were tough times indeed.
The ultimate answer for many came in the form of an IPO sale of publicly traded common equity in
their properties. This solution gave individual investors .control of the return of capital decision by the sale of
their shares. If the IPOs generated sufficient proceeds, it allowed the operators to pay off their maturing
debt, free themselves of personal recourse obligations, delever their portfolios, maintain control of their
management fee streams, and avoid capital gains taxes. The key was that, absent extreme leverage, these
assets provided a relatively predictable cash flow to investors. With a dash of transparency, a pinch of good
corporate governance, a slick road show, and some luck, investors just might buy Ms. Moy’s IPO offering in
pursuit of a low leveraged, relatively predictable cash stream.
So Ms. Moy rolled all of her property partnerships into a new entity, Moy REIT. She then
(successfully) sold $60 million in publicly traded common equity claims in this new entity via an IPO, with the
proceeds designated to pay off the $56 million of maturing debt, and roughly $4 million in IPO fees.
How did this solve Ms. Moy’s problem of salvaging her slim (and questionable) equity stake? After
all, the IPO’s $4 million in fees were in excess of her theoretical $2 million equity sliver. The answer was that
since she did not lose control of her properties to either third party buyers or foreclosing lenders, she still
owned the fee stream associated with managing these assets. This management fee stream, which would
become valueless as assets were sold or foreclosed, was rolled into Moy REIT, valued at a multiple of its fee
stream. At the time, her management fees were roughly $650,000 (5% of property revenue) per year. At a 7x
multiple, Ms. Moy’s fee stream was worth roughly $4.6 million. Thus, in exchange for her contribution of
this fee stream to Moy REIT, Ms. Moy received a 2% (for example) equity ownership of Moy REIT. Moy
REIT was now an internally managed public company, with Ms. Moy as Chairperson and CEO, for which
she would be compensated with a package of salary, bonus, and stock options. She agreed not to compete
with Moy REIT, and that she would not sell her equity ownership for a period of 12 months.

Thus, although the IPO drained $4 million in value due to IPO fees, it liquefied the value of Moy’s
management fee stream, allowed all maturing debt to be repaid in a timely manner, and freed Ms. Moy of her
personal guarantees. In addition, she held a well paid job with a company that was sufficiently deleveraged to
take aggressive advantage of her still distressed brethren. And no capital gains taxes were triggered in the
process.
You may be looking at Moy REIT’s ownership structure and wondering why she would settle for 2%
(in this example) REIT ownership, when she formerly owned 100% of her properties, and 100% of the
management company which owned the fee stream associated with managing these properties. The answer is
that 2% of something is a lot more than 100% of nothing. Plus, as an employee, she receives annual grants of
restricted shares and stock options, and 2 % of the dividends paid by the now deleveraged company.
Additionally, if she structured the UPREIT correctly, Ms. Moy deferred paying the capital gains taxes that
would have been due had she declared bankruptcy or sold the properties, as these taxes are deferred until Ms.
Moy sells her ownership in Moy REIT.
Post-IPO, Moy REIT could obtain a $16 million corporate line of credit from the lenders to whom
Ms. Moy had endeared herself, by repaying her debt while many others were defaulting. With this line of
credit, Moy REIT could purchase an additional $16 million in real estate and still maintain a 50% leverage

168

D R . P ETER LI N N EMAN

CHAPTER 18

level. When Moy REIT reached roughly 50% debt, it would issue a secondary equity offering to pay down
the line of credit, restoring borrowing capacity on the line of credit for further acquisitions.
To be sure, the process of Ms. Moy’s IPO was hardly a walk in the park. She spent roughly a year
preparing it, and was at risk of its failure to raise sufficient funds to repay the maturing debt.

But, upon

success, she was in an enviable position relative to other debt laden property owners. Post-IPO, she went to
these distressed owners and offered to purchase their properties in exchange for tax deferred equity
ownership interests in Moy REIT, allowing them to exit gracefully without triggering capital gain taxes, while
avoiding the costs, uncertainties, and headaches of either bankruptcy or their own public offerings. When
Moy REIT acquired these properties for ownership interests in Moy REIT, it also generally assumed the debt
on the properties. To retire this debt, Moy REIT either used its line of credit or floated additional equity.
Both routes were relatively fast, cheap, and easy, because Moy REIT was a modestly leveraged public
company with a transparent balance sheet. Also, since secondary equity offerings are much faster and
cheaper than IPOs, Moy REIT had a cost advantage over distressed owners contemplating survival via their
own IPOs. The main challenge facing Moy REIT was execution. And managing a public real estate
company in a rapidly changing market meant that if you screwed up, you became prey to a bigger and sawier
fish.

REITS
All companies attempt to minimize their tax liabilities. From a tax perspective, non-real estate
companies are basically C-corporations or partnerships. In a C-corp, corporate net income is taxed at the
corporate level. Whatever money a C-corp has after paying corporate taxes can be retained or distributed as
desired. If a C-corp decides to pay a portion of these funds in dividends, the shareholders (if they are taxable
entities) pay income taxes on their dividend income. This is referred to as double taxation, as the government
taxes profits at both the corporate and the shareholder level. If no dividend is paid, only a single corporate
tax is paid.
Alternatively, partnerships do not pay taxes at the corporate level, but instead pass through all tax
liabilities directly to the owners. If the property earns taxable income, the owners will be taxed pro rata on
these profits, regardless of whether this income is distributed to the owners. Since there is no corporate tax
payment, these structures are single taxation structures. The problem with these vehicles is that if the firm
retains the earnings, the owners have a tax liability with no distribution to pay the taxes. As a result, these
structures avoid double taxation, but owners can owe taxes without receiving any income.
The REIT tax structure offers the possibility of avoiding corporate taxes without generating tax
liabilities for owners, absent income distributions. This sounds perfect, right? So why isn’t every company in
America a REIT? The answer is that to qualify as a REIT under the tax code, a company must satisfy a long
litany of operating restrictions. Two of the most important operating restrictions are that the firm must be
primarily in the business of owning and operating real estate, and must pay out at least 90% of its taxable
income in dividends.
Who is impacted by the first restriction? Think about homebuilders (or builders in general). To
qualify as a REIT, homebuilders cannot sell the homes they build, as in order to qualify, they must own and
operate their properties (i.e., homes). However, a homebuilder is in the business of building and selling, not
owning and operating, homes. Similarly, developers rarely choose to be REITs, as most developers are in the
business of building and selling their properties upon stabilization. In addition, the combination of relatively
high debt levels and minimal taxable income during developing generates little taxable income to provide
shelter from corporate taxes. Therefore, there are generally few advantages and many operating drawbacks in
REIT status for a developer.

169

CHAPTER 18

DR. PETER LIN NEMAN

Since a REIT’s primary business must be owning and operating real estate, this tax structure is not
available to most companies. For example, General Motors owns a lot of real estate, but that is not their
primary business. If General Motors put their real estate in a separate and independent subsidiary that owned
and operated those facilities, the subsidiary could qualify as a REIT. But GM cannot qualify as a REIT.
To qualify as a REIT, the firm cannot be in the business of trading real estate. The definition of
“trading” is a bit ambiguous, as a REIT is allowed to sell some of its assets from time to time. The restriction
can be problematic. For example, a REIT may want to purchase 12 of the 45 properties in a portfolio being
sold, but the remaining 33 properties may not fit their strategy. Flowever, to purchase the 12 desirable
properties, it must purchase the entire portfolio, because that is what is being sold. If they acquire the entire
portfolio and sell the 33 undesirable properties as quickly as possible, they may endanger their REIT status, as
the IRS may view these sales as a trading business.
If a company loses its qualified status, the IRS can demand back taxes, interest on those taxes, and
perhaps penalties. The company will also be barred from becoming a REIT for at least five years. This
restriction is designed to prohibit firms from constandy switching status to minimize taxes.
Another major operating restriction is that a REIT must distribute 90% of its taxable income as
dividends. Elence, while it does not pass through its tax liability, a REIT must distribute a large portion of
available cash flow to shareholders. Shareholders are then taxed (to the extent that they are taxable entities)
on this dividend income. Remember that a REIT’s taxable income will generally be notably lower than cash
available for distribution, due, among other reasons, to depreciation. As a result, a REIT can generally
distribute as little as 50-70% of its available funds and still comply with the 90% of taxable income dividend
restriction.
As a real estate operator, why might you dislike the REIT dividend restriction? Because in order to
grow your business, you need the capital being distributed as dividends. As a result, you pay accountants to
calculate how much you must pay out in dividends to remain a REIT, and then you have to pay investment
bankers to raise money to grow your business. That’s expensive! Even worse, if you select REIT tax status
and unknowingly violate one of the many operating restrictions, not only did you pay out money you could
have used to fund growth, but the government still collects corporate taxes, interest on those taxes, and
penalties. As a result, firms generally maintain a cushion with respect to the REIT operating restrictions. In
short, REITs achieve single taxation, but at a price.
