SUPPLEMENTAL III

DR. PETER LINN EM AN

Supplemental III
Careers In Real Estate
Students frequently tell me, “I’m really interested in real estate. So which investment bank should I
go to work for?” I always respond that while investment banking is a great career path, there are many other
career options in real estate. To say you want to be in real estate is as vague as saying you want to be in
medicine. Do you want to be a cardiologist, pediatrician, nurse, professor, pharmaceutical sales rep, CFO for
a healthcare company, hospital administrator, technician, researcher, or dentist? The career options discussed
in this chapter are in no way comprehensive, but offer a glimpse into the variety of real estate career paths
available. Opportunity funds and REITs are not discussed here, as they are described in detail elsewhere in
the book.
Research: Real estate, like any industry, constantly seeks quality information. Thus, the business has many
research niches. Equity research teams at major investment houses analyze market trends and company
information to evaluate publicly traded real estate companies. Other research specialists, such as Linneman
Associates, Torto Wheaton, and Rosen Consulting Group, provide analysis on how macro and micro events
impact the real estate industry. Firms like REIS provide market rent, building permit, vacancy, and other
detailed market data. Sometimes research firms are divisions of banks or money managers.
Junior level researchers gather and analyze data, work on surveys, and compile data in easy to
understand formats. Writing skills are highly sought after by research firms.
Brokers and Leasing Agents: Brokers are the plankton of the industry, in that they are everywhere. They
range from mom and pop shops to huge public corporations. These licensed real estate professionals assess
local market values, market properties, and advise clients on the purchase, leasing, and sale of properties.
Frequently, corporate users engage brokers as their representatives, to identify the best office space,
warehouse space, retail space, or housing for their needs. They are also lease space for owners. Brokers
generally work for fees based primarily upon successful deal execution.
Major firms include Grubb & Ellis, CB Richard Ellis, Cushman & Wakefield, Trammell Crow, and
Staubach. In addition, there are many local brokers and agents. Also, major investment banks such as
Goldman Sachs and Morgan Stanley perform some of these services, as do Eastdil and Secured Capital.
The advantage of working for a broker is that you learn about the demand side of real estate, as great
brokers understand what different firms want, and what market participants value. For example, a financial
services firm looking for office space in Manhattan has substantially different space needs than a major law or
industrial firm. In addition, each property, and each suite in property, has its own unique usage aspects.
Good brokers understand these elements, and seek to match these traits with customers. No two markets or
properties are identical, and a good broker understands who best fits what.
A junior person in this industry works on reports, marketing materials, research, leasing and space
use plans, and assists in structuring leases and property sales. In this business, writing skills are useful, and
knowledge of programs such as PowerPoint, Argus, and Excel are valued.
Investment Banks: This sector serves the capital needs of REITs, opportunity funds, private operators, etc.
They raise debt and equity, provide advisory work on mergers and acquisitions, and advise on restructuring
situations. Some banks also have dedicated real estate investment funds.

238

DR. P E TE R LINNEM A N

SUPPLEMENTAL

III

Often, when REITs and other large real estate firms float debt, they use an investment bank. This
debt may be used to finance a large acquisition or the purchase of another firm, or to refinance existing debt.
Investment banks use their sales networks to place both secured and unsecured real estate debt, frequently
trading in the debt’s after-market. The equity raising process is much the same. When an investment bank is
engaged to raise capital, the bank’s real estate group works with the lawyers and the capital markets group to
structure the offering, which is then placed through an equity syndicate that coordinates road shows with
institutional investors.
Investment banks also perform advisory work, including fairness opinions, and frequently act as
brokers for major property sales. For example, the bank’s technology group will be advising on a merger
which creates the need to dispose of surplus office space, and the bank’s real estate group may be brought in
to market this property. The bank’s main expertise lies in their knowledge of capital markets.
A junior person at an investment bank creates pitch books, prepares PowerPoint presentations, and
constructs detailed financial models. The hours are long and tedious.

Institutional investors: Insurance companies, pension funds, endowments, and investment companies have
money to invest. Sometimes their assets are matched with their liabilities. For example, a pension fund has
the relatively certain long-term liabilities associated with current retirees, and the less certain liability
associated with current employees who will one day be retirees.
Institutional investors invest in real estate for both its diversification and return attributes. Some
institutional investors invest a portion of their assets in higher risk vehicles such as opportunity funds, as well
as REITs, individual properties, mortgages, and CMBS.
Junior level people in this field work on asset management, due diligence, gathering information on
investments, measuring performance, preparing reports, and selecting managers.

Commercial Banks: Commercial banks underwrite loans, frequently acting as an intermediary between an
issuer of securities and the investing public. Commercial banks also engage in lending activities, sometimes
holding their loans, other times syndicating them, and sometimes packaging them as CMBS offerings. Since
the repeal of the Glass-Steagall Act in 1999, there has been a substantial integration of investment banks and
major commercial banks. Elowever, local commercial banks still function largely as lenders.
Given their short-term liabilities (deposits), commercial banks are typically the primary source of
construction loans. They also provide 3-7-year loans, particularly to local property owners. These loans are
frequently provided to developers to fund the construction and stabilization of new properties.
Entry level people at commercial banks assist in the due diligence process, checking financial
projections, construction timelines, etc. Since many bank loans are secured by recourse to personal assets,
they often analyze the personal balance sheets of the borrowers.

Developers: Developers range from large national firms like Forest City, Hines, and the Related Company,
to small local developers. Most specialize in a single property type, such as retail, hotel, residential,
commercial, or resort, as each region and property type requires different skill sets, tenant relationships, and
local connections. For example, a great condo developer knows how to create a buzz about his product, and
is able to market the units quickly. Some developers are experts at controlling project costs, and others are
great at marketing, while others are masters of design. Land developers focus on pre-selling lots and
obtaining the necessary zoning approvals, while mall developers pre-lease space, using their rolodexes and
relationships with retail tenants. Single family home development is basically a manufacturing process, where
land is merely production inventory.

239

DR.

SUPPLEMENTAL

P E T E R LIN N E M A N

III

The best developers use their core competencies to get an edge on the competition, and have the
political and organizational savvy to get through the regulatory maze efficiently. Many developers
subcontract architects, lawyers, general contractors, brokers, or a marketing team, adding value by efficiently
managing these diverse outsourced components. Others are more integrated, performing many or all of these
functions in-house.
The developer’s job is about both taking and eliminating risks. Developers seek out viable deals.
While a development case may be assigned every couple of weeks in a real estate class, most developers will
only find a viable development deal once every year or two. After finding such a deal, the developer lines up
capital from both equity investors and lenders. The developer continually fine tunes building plans, as
markets, tenants, and regulatory and engineering challenges evolve. Upon completion, developers must lease
space. There is often no precise timetable, and the developer must coordinate and juggle all of these moving
parts.
Junior level people at development firms create financial models, and aid in the market analysis and
due diligence processes. They will also assist in preparing marketing materials for tenants and capital
providers, as well as with presentations to planning authorities.
Architects and Planners: People think of such architects as I.M. Pei, Helmut Jahn, Kohn Pederson Fox, or
Frank Gehry as creators of wonderful works of art. But most architects are in the business of designing
usable and profitable space. Good architects design commercial space from the inside out, rather than from
the outside in. While the exterior of the building is a great advertisement for the property and the architect,
the layout of the interior space must effectively meet the needs of space users to be successful. Layout and
design vary across property types, users, and markets. It is the architect’s job to make sure that a usable,
flexible, and efficient structure is designed, which complies with zoning codes and other local regulations.
Architects often specialize in different components of a project, such as interiors, schematics, landscaping,
mechanicals, or facades.
This field attracts many young “artists” who quickly discover that it is a highly competitive business,
where one prospers by executing the creative visions of developers efficiently, rather than by making personal
artistic statements.
General Contracting Firms: General contractors coordinate construction and project logistics, creating and
supervising schedules and budgets for each element of the construction process. For example, should the
carpenters do their work before or after the electrical contractors? The job of general contractors is to keep
the construction process running on time, coordinating construction details with the developer, architect, and
subcontractors. General contractors often have a specialty in one or more aspects of the construction
process. They must plan for unforeseen delays and costs, and conceptualize all of the details required to
complete the property in a timely and cost-effective manner.
In this field, young people work on budgets, monitor project timing and costs, and facilitate
negotiations with vendors and subcontractors.
Lawyers: The real estate industry is filled with “deal guys” who buy, sell, and lease properties. This requires
the assistance of many lawyers to draft, finalize, and negotiate detailed documents. Sometimes lawyers have
an expertise in specialties like historic tax credits, environmental, zoning, leasing, or construction.
Lawyers create the documents which allow a developer to sell historic tax credits, or purchase a
property. Lawyers also draft partnership agreements so that liability, taxes, and returns are distributed among
partners as agreed. For example, non-taxable entities such as college endowments cannot use tax credits, so
complex legal structures are frequently crafted, which most efficiently allocate these benefits among investors.

240

D R.

P ETE R L I N N EM A N

SUPPLEMENTAL

III

To be on the legal side of the real estate business, a J.D. is generally required. A young lawyer will do
legal research, work on due diligence, and assist in contract drafting.
Concluding Thought: These career descriptions barely scratch the surface of the possibilities in real estate.
Never forget that every building and vacant piece of land is owned by someone, and that owners (including
government agencies) must operate, finance, and report on the usage of the property. Real estate is
ubiquitous in our world, and the career paths are literally endless. Don’t limit yourself to investment banks
simply because your friends are working for these firms.

241

SUPPLEMENTAL IV

DR . PETER LI NNEMAN

ARGUS Financial Software
Authors:
John Lim, Director of Client Services
Lynn Byrne, Senior Consultant
Realm Business Solutions, Inc.
WHAT IS ARGUS™?
ARGUS, by Realm Business Solutions, is the industry standard for cash flow analysis, with over
15,000 users in over 39 countries. This user-friendly application is designed to be comprehensive, yet has the
ability to handle sophisticated analytical requirements in asset transactions.
ARGUS can be used to model most commercial real estate types in all aspects of the life cycle: from
initial acquisition, through development, to lease-up and disposition. This application is also used to value
assets, view partnership structures, and analyze debt financing.
Developers, property and leasing brokers, equity and debt investors, construction and permanent
lenders, fee appraisers, bank appraisers, asset managers, tax assessors, mortgage bankers, investment bankers,
and corporate real estate executives find that ARGUS offers flexibility and functionality. In addition, non¬
commercial users, including municipal and school finance departments and bond rating agencies, use ARGUS
to determine future revenues from non-property tax income, such as recreational use fees.
ARGUS uses detailed monthly calculations to analyze the terms of the individual leases currently in
place, the assumptions of future leasing activity, and the rollovers and renewals of each lease as it expires.
Additional information about the property can be entered to account for miscellaneous revenues, expenses,
capital expenditures, and debt. The resulting cash flow model can be used for long-term budget projections.
Each year’s estimated cash flows can be utilized subsequently for comparison to the actual cash flows from a
property management system. ARGUS can also be used for the evaluation and negotiation of lease
proposals, or to help develop leasing strategies.
Using an application like ARGUS reduces the possibility for human error in building your financial
model. Investors know how to work the deal, but often the calculator they use is a spreadsheet (or even a
less technologically advanced tool) on the fly. This produces a need for duplicating a basic spreadsheet for
multiple projects. Further, programming errors are a huge problem for the investor.
Real estate offers a large variety of properties and leases. ARGUS handles calculations from simple
base rent to complicated CAM reimbursement pools. But the accuracy of the calculations is only as good as
the data that is entered. While ARGUS cannot determine the quality of this data, it helps the systematic
process of inputting assumptions. Data can be imported (and exported) from other systems, allowing for
greater accuracy and speed in data input
ARGUS offers a common platform for doing business, as the value of a financial model is greatly
improved when you can easily share results with others involved in the transaction. With its large user base,
the ARGUS format is widely familiar. Much like Word™ and Excel™ are platforms for letters and
spreadsheets, ARGUS is an accepted platform for real estate.

Williams Center is a 50,000 square-foot office building located at 10 Brook Lane, Houston, Texas.
The subject was constructed in 1987. A 5-year discounted cash flow will be used to value the property,
because income is expected to vary over the next few years.

The date of valuation is January 1, 2004. Inflation will be calculated on a calendar basis at 3%. The

D R . P E T E R LINN E M A N

SUPPLEMENTAL IV

MISCELLANEOUS REVENUES AND EXPENSES

In addition to base rent, revenue is also derived from leasing roof space for an antenna. This
miscellaneous revenue is estimated initially at $15,000 per year, increasing at 3% per annum.

The reimbursable operating expenses are:
Real Estate Taxes are estimated at $1.65 per square foot per year in year 1. Thereafter, the tax liability is

trended at 2% per year for inflationary expectations. Taxes are a 100% fixed expense.

245

SUPPLEMENTAL IV

D R . PETER L 1 N N EM AN

Insurance coverage, including fire insurance, extended coverage, liability, and other space coverage required by
a property of the subject type, is estimated at $0.14 pey square foot per year. This expense is increased
annually at 3%, consistent with inflationary expectations, and is a fixed expense. Since the general model
inflation factor is used, no specific inflation factor need be entered here. This is also the case for many other
categories.
Utilities are estimated at $1.85 per square foot in year 1. This is a variable expense. 25% of this expense
would be incurred if the property were vacant. This expense is expected to increase at 3% per year over the
holding period.
Repairs and Maintenance are estimated at $0.98 per square foot in year 1. This includes all interior and exterior
maintenance and repairs and janitorial expenses for the building. It is estimated that the property would still
incur 35% of this expense if the building were totally vacant. These expenses are projected to grow at the
general inflation rate of 3%.
Grounds and Security typically include all salary-related items for grounds (including landscaping), maintenance,
and security, as well as outside contract services. The grounds and security expense is forecast at $0.83 per
square foot per year. This is a fixed expense, trended at 3%, based on anticipated inflationary trends.

The subject’s roof (shown above) is partially defective, and will need major repairs by year 3.
Property management is currently projecting this repair for July of year 3. Current estimates from area
contractors indicate the expense will be $55,000 at that time.

Suite 200 is currently vacant, but a new tenant, Austin Computers, is scheduled to occupy the space
in two months. The subject property is 76% occupied. Currendy, 12,000 square feet are vacant. The
building has an excellent location, and is a very good quality Class B office building. The vacant area is
available at the beginning of the analysis, with absorption projected to begin in July 2004.

247

SUPPLEMENTAL IV

DR. P E T E R LI N N E M A N

In the analysis of the property, an absorption rate of 2,000 square feet per month is deemed
reasonable for the vacant areas, considering the amount and location of the space at the subject property.
Lease terms are assumed to be for five years, and rent will be 100% of current market rent. Reimbursements
will be over a base year expense stop. To prepare this vacant space for occupancy, a $15.00/square foot
tenant improvement allowance is applied. Also, a 5% leasing commission is assigned to each 2000 squarefoot office.

Market leasing assumptions are standard for space that is less than 20,000 square feet Quoted rates
and actual recent lease terms at comparable office buildings were analyzed to determine current market rent.
The building has two classifications of space. One is for tenants of less than 20,000 square feet, and the other
is for tenants of more than 20,000 square feet.
For tenants with less than 20,000 square feet, the data is as follows: renewal probability is assumed to
be 50%; market rent is $18 per square foot per year for new tenants and $17 per square foot per year for
renewing tenants; months vacant is four months downtime between leases; tenant improvements are $15
square foot allowance for new tenants and $5 per square foot allowance for renewing tenants; leasing

248

D R . P E T E R LINNE M A N

SUPPLEMENTAL IV

commissions initial/new leasing commissions are estimated at 5%; renewing tenants require a leasing
commission of 3%; reimbursement method is base year stop; and the lease term length is five years.
For tenants with more than 20,000 square feet, the data is as follows: the renewal probability is
assumed to be 75%; market rent is $15 per square foot per year for new tenants and renewing tenants;
months vacant is six months downtime between leases; tenant improvements are $20 per square foot
allowance for new tenants and $5 per square foot allowance for renewing tenants; leasing commissions
initial/new leasing commissions are estimated at 5%; renewing tenants require a leasing commission of 3%;
reimbursement method is base year stop; and the lease term length is 10 years. The less than 20,000
speculative lease assumptions are shown.

VIEWING REPORTS
ARGUS produces a wide variety of property, tenant, comparison, and audit reports, including:
Summary of Cash Flow from Operations; Schedule of Expense Reimbursement Revenue; Market Leasing
Assumptions; Resale and Terminal Value Summary; Rate of Return & Present Value Summary; Individual
Tenant Cash Flow & Summary; Occupied Area Measures; Income Statements; Depreciations Schedules; and
numerous supporting schedules for each detailed line item found on the Summary Reports.
ARGUS also produces comparison reports showing much of this detail on a per square foot basis.
Further, ARGUS produces an internal rate of return, a yearly cash-on-cash return, average occupancy rations,
weighted average market rates, weighted average effective rents, and many other useful ratios and
benchmarks.

YIELD ON THE INVESTMENT

The building was purchased for $4,500,000. The reversion value in year 5 is estimated by capitalizing
the sixth year’s net operating income. The property is in a good location, and is one of the better quality
office buildings in the area. Therefore, a terminal capitalization rate of 10.5% is considered appropriate.
Sales adjustments, including commissions and closing costs, are estimated to be 3% of the gross sales and

There is a secured loan on this property. The $3,000,000 loan starts at the beginning of the analysis,
amortizing over 30 years at an 8% interest rate. A 1% origination fee is included on this note. The subject is
a modern office building, constructed of very good quality materials, and well maintained. It is located in
close proximity to major roadways. This area has been experiencing high occupancies (91%+). The subject
is below stabilized occupancy. Given these considerations, an annual end-point discount rate of 11.25% is
considered appropriate.

The supporting schedules show the cash flow results by tenants or tenant groups. These reports are

CLOSING THOUGHT
Having done the calculations to achieve a pro forma of the property, the user has the tools for a
better understanding of the property. While ARGUS provides the form and the calculations to model the
property quickly and accurately, the analysis still rests squarely on the user’s shoulders.
ARGUS has the ability to take existing assumed data, base rents, reimbursement schedules, some
miscellaneous income, and, to a large extent, the operating expenses of a building, to give a good
representation of the property as it stands today. But the market conditions and assumptions for the
projected years are made by the analyst. The saying “garbage in, garbage-out” applies here, as it does with any
other application.
ARGUS gives the user the ability to manipulate the assumptions and change conditions, as well as
transparency and portability of data during a transaction. The power of its calculations lays the foundation
for negotiations. But ultimately, it is only a tool. Risk and reward decisions are still made by humans, not
machines.

By 1995, the property had appreciated greatly in price, and it

The bank loan is interest-only, matures January 1, 2005, and bears a 9% interest rate.

This bank loan contains a provision that if the appraised value of Regency is less than 120%
of the note, the loan can be called by the bank at any time. In addition, the bank loan
forbids any other debt being taken on by the trust, so long as the loan is outstanding. The

until the bank loan obligations are satisfied.

Case: Build to Suit
Over the past seven years, you have developed 12 build-to-suit facilities for Convenient Marts.
These facilities are generally located on strip center outparcels, are roughly 11,000 square feet, and are
completely occupied by a Convenient Marts store. These properties have all been located along the main
thoroughfares of suburban Philadelphia.
The typical lease terms on these deals have been:
•

A 10-year lease term

•

Rent is triple net

•

Rent has provided roughly a 350-basis-point spread over the 10-year Treasury rate when the deal was
signed

•

A 10-year renewal option

•

Convenient Marts may purchase the property (Convenient Marts has the option) at any time for “fair
market value”

•

An option to purchase the property in year 10 (and year 20, if the lease option is renewed) at a 10%
discount to “fair market value”

•

A right of first refusal with respect to both lease and purchase as long as the lease is in effect.

The typical Convenient Marts store is a plain “box”, and petroleum product sales are prohibited in the leases.
Over the past decade, Convenient Marts has expanded in the suburban areas of the major
Midatlantic state metropolitan areas. This publicly traded company has been rated BBB+ or better for the
past seven years.
Convenient Marts has recently embarked upon an aggressive expansion campaign, targeting the
suburban areas of secondary Midatlantic cities for new stores. As a result of this expansion effort, S&P has
placed Convenient Mart’s debt on a “credit watch”, expressing concern that this effort may result in lower
quality cash flows and an increased debt burden.
You have sold 10 of the 12 Convenient Mart stores you have developed. In each case, you sold them
within 18 months of completion, for an average cap rate that has a spread over the 10-year Treasury of about
210 basis points. The two properties you have not sold have been completed with in the past 12 months.
You anticipate that these properties will each sell for a spread of about 200 basis points over the 10-year
Treasury during the next year.
These properties you have developed for Convenient Marts have taken you as little as 10 months and
as much as 16 months to complete. Typically, it requires about 12 months to develop one of these stores.
Convenient Marts has approached you and asked you to play a major development role in their
expansion effort. Specifically, they have indicated that they would like you to develop stores for them in the

D R . P E TER L 1NNE M A N

Allentown/Bethlehem, Pennsylvania area. They plan to add about 10 stores in this region over the next 3-4
years, and want you to be their developer in this effort.
Their first store site in this market is along the main suburban throughway in suburban Allentown. It
is for a 10,000 square-foot store, with appropriate parking, ingress/egress, and signage requirements.
Convenient Marts’ real estate committee has approved the following non-negotiable (take it or walk) deal
terms:

•

10-year lease

•

10-year renewal option

•

Option to purchase at any time at “fair market value”

has no amortization and is prepayable without penalty. You will also receive a development fee of 5% of
hard costs, plus approval, planning, and design costs.
Initial discussions with your lender and local planning officials lead you to anticipate no unusual
problems with the project. The equity required for this project is available, but would absorb about 30% of
your equity capacity.
Should you undertake this development? You are to write a 3-page business memo to your board,

The property is unlikely to support any ground level retail.

Construction will probably take about one year.

11 % equity IRR.

•

They will allow you to take a development fee of 4% of construction costs, with half paid on a
pro rata basis to construction outlays, and the other half in a subordinated equity position.

Finally, though you hate to admit it, if you undertake this project, you will have to work on it full-time.

SUPPLEMENTAL V

Case: Industrial Opportunities in Mexico:
Time to buy or time to sell?
INTRODUCTION:
You are an advisor to:
a)

an opportunity fund;

b)

a REIT;

c)

a pension fund;

d)

a lender.

Analyze this case from the perspective of the four types of investors listed above. Your client has retained
your services to determine whether it should become a player in the industrial real estate sector of Mexico.
Your client has been hearing a lot over the last 12 months about this opportunity, and wants you to study the
country, the sector, and its current players, to determine whether this is a real opportunity, and if so, what
strategy it should follow to invest in it.

Specifically, your client is looking for your recommendations to

include an assessment of the risks of participating in this potential opportunity, and whether these risks are in
line with those customarily taken by an investor of its profile. They would also like to know which part of the
real estate cycle this sector is in.

Finally and most importantly, they are looking for you to recommend a

strategy that will maximize their returns vis-a-vis their risk profile, and how much money, if any, they should
allocate to the initiative.

THE MEXICAN INDUSTRIAL SECTOR:
The Mexican maquiladora program was created by the Mexican government back in the late 1960s in Ciudad
Juarez, Mexico, to lure foreign multinationals to invest in manufacturing operations in Mexico, and create
badly needed jobs in the country. Foreign companies wishing to set up industrial facilities for the production
of goods for exports are exempted from import duties on any raw materials, capital goods, and any other
inputs to production that enter the

country temporarily, to be incorporated into finished products that are

later exported back. Additionally, especially pre-1997, companies were lured by the ability to hire relatively
low-cost Mexican labor. These production facilities are called maquiladoras, and this sector of the Mexican
real estate market has witnessed enormous growth in recent years, following NAFTA’s passage.

The

northern border area of Mexico is dotted with industrial parks and manufacturing facilities, doing mostly
labor intensive production of export goods: stamping circuit boards; assembling printers and computers;
integrating automotive subassemblies into finished cars; etc.

As of March 2002, there are over 3,230

maquiladoras employing over a million people in Mexico.
The early and mid-1990s saw constant double-digit growth of the industrial space base of most of the border
cities. The labor cost began escalating, and labor turnover rates increased. New cities deeper into the country
emerged as new maquiladora towns (Guadalajara, Queretaro, San Luis Potosi, etc.). This opened the door for
these production platforms to be better located to supply the local Mexican market with its products.

265

SUPPLEMENTAL V

DR. PETER LIN NEMAN

Lately, as supply chains integrate themselves closely around the “just in time” concept, companies are
attracted to relocate to Mexican cities where clusters of suppliers to the same industry (i.e., automotive,
electronics, home appliances, etc.) have established themselves.

The reduction in transportation costs and

time to market, as well as the increasingly shorter life span of technology-related products, represents an
increasingly important reason to manufacture items quickly and in a geographic location close to its client
base.

The increasing labor costs of some of the first maquiladora cities have been more than offset by the

savings in transportation costs and obsolescence costs, as compared with bringing some components from
lower labor cost countries in Asia, paying shipping costs, and suffering lead times of 4-6 weeks.

BACKGROUND:
Real estate development opportunities in the industrial sector have been abundant over the last eight years,
mostly due to the increase in absorption of this type of space, as a consequence of the explosive growth of
foreign trade between Mexico and United States.

When NAFTA took effect in 1994, Mexican exports to the U.S. were mainly oil-related products, raw

manufacturing goods.

changed quickly,

COMPETITIVE ARENA:
Mexico’s industrial base, as measured by the number of maquiladoras established in the country, grew by 71%
(from 2,013 to 3,450) over the period between 1991 and 2001.

Although Ciudad Juarez, Tijuana, and

Monterrey continue to be the top three maquila cities of the country, emerging cities like Guadalajara,
Reynosa, and Queretaro have increasingly taken market share from other more traditional cities.
266

DR. PETER LIN NEMAN

SUPPLEMENTAL V

Over the years, the opportunities in the industrial sector have attracted national and international players to
the different sectors of the real estate chain. A large enough pool of qualified architecture and engineering
firms, brokers, general contractors, and developers now exists, and although entitlement risk and
infrastructure availability are always challenging, the overall development risk has been significantly reduced
from a few years ago, when most foreign investors would not have achieved the comfort level to invest in this
sector.
Developers like Gaccion (gaccion.com), Hines (hines.com), FINSA (finsa.net), CPA
(cpamericas.com) and Prologis (prologis.com) have put together sizable and attractive industrial portfolios
over the years, which have attracted international institutional capital from investors such as The Peabody
Group, Morgan Stanley/Dean Witter, AIG, Equity International, and GE Capital. These portfolios have also
attracted lenders who have tried, in most cases unsuccessfully, to penetrate the sector.
Over this same time frame, market caps have been decreasing, as a reflection of reduced development risk,
formation of a more liquid capital market for the sector, availability of equity and debt, country risk
reductions, etc.
Market vacancies in most industrial cities are below 5%, but most come from 5+ years of below 2%.
Industrial absorption has experienced a deceleration consistent with the one the U.S. economy has suffered.
As Mexico and the U.S. further integrate their economies (80% of Mexican exports go to U.S., and 60% of its
imports come from there), Mexico is fast approaching Canada as the number one trading partner of the U.S.

TYPICAL INDUSTRIAL DEAL STRUCTURE:
The typical structure of an industrial deal in Mexico is as follows:
•

5-10-year lease in U.S. dollars, with annual CPI increases

•

70-80% debt, with interest rates of LIBOR plus 300-400 basis points (depending on credit of tenant);
debt can be fixed by purchasing an interest rate SWAP, which costs approximately 300 basis points

•

Debt terms are for 5-10 years, with minimum 2-year lockouts and 15+-year amortization schedules.

•

Most tenants are American, European, or Asian multinationals opening operations in Mexico, and
most provide corporate guaranties in their leases.

•

Overall, the lease structure and language of the lease contracts are very similar to the ones these
corporations are sign in the U.S. or in other countries where they have international operations.

In

the case of Mexico, most include arbitration clauses in U.S. courts to avoid lengthy and uncertain
legal processes in Mexico.
•

Design and construction standards are very similar to those of a U.S. industrial asset.

YOUR ASSIGNMENT:
In preparing recommendations for your clients, take into consideration the different risk profile of each one,
the depth and liquidity of the industrial sector in Mexico, the risk-adjusted returns of a typical industrial deal
in Mexico vs. the ones you could get in a similar industrial building in the U.S. or another country, geopolitics,
etc.
Although this case study has more to do with strategic thinking, based on historical and projected trends for
the industrial sector in Mexico, than with detailed numerical analysis, basic numbers must support your
recommendations.

FINAL EXAM
React: (10 points) NAV is a more accurate reflection of the true value of REITs for a long-term investor
than either book value or the current share price.
React: (10 points) The main advantage of a corporation leasing its real estate is that the lease payments
reduce taxable income, thereby increasing after-tax income.
React: (10 points) It is better to refinance a property whose value has risen substantially than to sell the
property, as a refinancing avoids the capital gains taxes which are due upon sale.
React: (10 points) Down cycles in real estate values and rents are less likely to occur in markets with strong
growth trends, as the presence of a strong growth trend means that any excess supply will be
absorbed rapidly.
React: (10 points) A pool of CMBS is a superior investment to a pool of whole mortgages, because the
CMBS securities are rated by the major rating agencies.
React: (10 points) In the case of a property with a ground lease, the landowner will never subordinate the
ground ownership position to the mortgage loan on the structure, because in the case of a default by
the structure owner, the property will be lost to the mortgage lender.

The pro forma sales price for the retail center is $60 million at the end of year 6. The apartments

per unit.
Not included in the costs already mentioned is roughly $10 million during the first year of the

The developer has approached your opportunity fund to be the lead equity provider for this project.
They note that upon completion, the total project costs will be roughly $255 million. The developer
plans to borrow $155 million (the debt carry costs are included in the development costs described
above). They further note that after the first year, PH should be self-funding, as the profits from
sales of the completed residences should cover all future equity requirements. As a result, they
require roughly $60 million of initial equity (with the remainder of first-year costs being borrowed).
They conclude by saying that upon complete build-out and sale, the pro forma profit on PH over its
life is approximately $220 million on the original $60 million equity, and that profits on sales from
the residences should allow a return of all equity capital after approximately three years.
Having heard their presentation, you are impressed by their professionalism, the physical design of
PH, the site of the local market, demand and supply dynamics, the general reasonableness of their
cost and sales estimates, and their track record as developers of suburban garden apartments.
What (and why) will you recommend to your partners in terms of whether this development merits
the serious attention of your firm?

271

SUPPLEMENTAL VI

DR. P E T E R L I N N E M A N

MID-TERM EXAM
React: (10 points) Since you should use a discount rate reflective of the investment’s risk to discount cash
flows, the appropriate discount rate for developing an A-quality, 1 million square-foot office building
is about the same as the discount rate for an existing, fully leased 1 million square-foot office
building.

React: (10 points) An apartment building investment that provides a 35% expected IRR on equity is a more
attractive investment than an investment of an equal amount in a supermarket anchored strip
shopping center that provides a 12% IRR on equity.

React: (10 points) A loan with a 7% interest rate is more attractive than a loan with a 7.5% interest rate, as
long as both loans are of the same length and have the same amortization schedule.

React: (40 points) You are offered the opportunity to develop a 400,000 square-foot warehouse facility for
General Foods in suburban Philadelphia. Attracted by your expertise as a local warehouse
developer, General Foods has offered to sign a triple net lease for seven years at a rent of $2 million
per year. They also insist that at the end of the lease term, they have the option to purchase the
property at $22 million. Their final major condition is that the project be completed and ready for
occupancy in 11 months. If it is not complete by that time, the deal is void.
You estimate that the property will cost $20 million to complete (including all costs), and that you
should be able to complete it within 10 months if you commence construction immediately.

You

believe that you can obtain a construction cost guarantee that costs will not exceed $20.5 million.
You believe that you will be able to obtain a $14-15 million, 7-year loan with 25-year amortization, at
a fixed interest rate of 8%, plus a 50-basis-point fee.

You believe that you can close this loan 6-8

weeks from now.
You believe that your company can access approximately $8 million in equity, assuming that you can
successfully tap into appreciated equity positions in three existing properties without triggering
capital gains taxes on these positions. Your company will receive a development fee of roughly 3%
of project costs (which is included in your $20 million cost estimate).
Finally, vacancy rates in the market are approximately 4%, and gross rents in the market run $7-9 per
square foor, with operating expenses and taxes running $2-4 per square foor. Negotiations are over,
and it is time to make a decision.
Should you agree to develop the property? Give your reasons.

272

DR. PETER LIN NEMAN

SUPPLEMENTAL VI

FINAL EXAM
React: (10 Points)

Becoming a publicly traded real estate company in the form of a REIT makes a real

estate company 5-10% more valuable than if it remains private.

React: (10 Points) Corporate real estate ownership is greater in Europe than in the U.S., because European
managers have bigger egos.

React: (10 Points) A mortgage is more valuable as part of a CMBS offering than as a non-securitized
mortgage.

React: (10 Points) Real estate analysis uses cap rates to determine values rather than multiples, because real
estate is a physical, rather than financial, asset.

React: (10 Points) If a shopping center has 70% of its space leased for the next 30 years to a supermarket
anchor, the owner does not have to worry very much about local market conditions, because 70% of
the available space will be off the market for the next 30 years.

React: (10 Points) Debt always enhances the returns on equity, because the borrowing rate is lower than the
cash yield, and properties go up in value.

React: (50 Points) You are working for an active investment group which is very interested in an office
property in Tokyo. The property is 16 years old, well (but not spectacularly) located, with good
floor plates, and meets earthquake standards.
The property is available for $100 million (all payments will be in yen, but I’ll express them in dollars
to ease your headaches), which is a mere 20% of the value placed on the property in 1991. The
property needs about $20 million in improvements to make it competitive with new properties. The
property is currently vacant, as it was the headquarters of a recently liquidated bankrupt company.
At the all-in cost of $120 million, the property would have a cost equal to roughly 90% replacement
cost, though since land costs are 50-60% of replacement costs, and land prices continue to fall, it is
hard to be precise on this estimate.
The local rental market for quality properties is fairly strong, in spite of the weak Japanese economy,
as tenants continue to take advantage of low rents by moving out of poorly located and designed
properties into better properties. Leases in Tokyo are only two years in duration, and the tenant
typically pays triple net rents. Property values are at levels not seen since the early 1980s. Sales are
relatively frequent, but pricing is unpredictable, with American buyers paying 6-8% cap rates, and
Japanese firms occasionally paying 3-5% cap rates. Two REITs recently did IPOs at roughly 6% cap
rates, but their prices have fallen substantially since their IPOs.
Rents in Tokyo for quality properties continue to fall by 1-2% per year, as new construction
continues to generate a modest supply/demand imbalance. This situation, and the general Japanese
economic malaise, is expected to continue for the next several years.

273

DR. ? E T E R L I N N E M A N

SUPPLEMENTAL VI

You believe that after one year to complete the necessary improvements, you will be able to lease the
property for an NOI of $8.4 million (after usual reserves). You can borrow $100 million via a non¬
recourse loan with 5-year maturity, no points, 30-year amortization, and a fixed interest rate of 2%.
To eliminate all currency risks on your investment (let’s not worry about how this is done) will cost
$1.2 million per year.
Your target rate of return is roughly 17% on your equity for a 5-year hold. What do you think, and
why?

274

DR. PETER LIN N EM AN

SUPPLEMENTAL

VI

MIDTERM EXAM
React: (9 Points) If a buyer of a property says that they purchased it for a 9% cap rate, while the seller of the
same property says they sold it for an 8% cap rate, one of them must be lying.

React: (9 Points) Since the allowable depreciable life on an apartment building is 27.5 years, versus 39 years
for a shopping center, an apartment complex must have substantially lower annual capital
expenditure requirements than a shopping center.

React: (9 Points) NAV is the single most reliable metric of the value of a publicly traded real estate
company, as it indicates the breakup value of the company’s real estate assets.

React: (9 Points) A 3-year floating rate mortgage with no amortization, a 4.5% interest rate, a 50-basis-point
closing fee, and no prepayment penalty is a better loan than a 10-year loan with a fixed 6.0% interest
rate, a 20-year amortization schedule, a 75-basis-point closing fee, and a yield maintenance
requirement.

React: (34 Points) You have been approached by General Electric (GE) to develop a 150,000 square-foot
office facility for them in suburban Dallas. They will use the property for “back office” operations
and corporate training. After considerable negotiation, you have received their final non-negotiable
offer. They would lease the facility for six years, at a triple net annual rent of $2,250,000. The rent
would remain constant over the term of the lease. GE would also have the option to renew their
lease for an additional six years for $3,000,000 (triple net). In addition, they would have the option
to purchase the property at any time during their lease (including the renewal period, if exercised) for
the price of $29 million. They insist that the property be completed by 11 months from today.
Failure to achieve this completion date would relieve them from agreement.
You estimate that the project will cost $25 million (all costs included), and will take 9.5 months from
today to complete. You feel that, due to the presence of GE as a tenant, you will be able to secure a
$20 million loan during the next three months, at a 6% interest rate for three years with no
amortization. You have $2 million in cash available to start the project immediately, and believe that
during the next two months, you will be able to access the remaining $3 million of required equity
either by refinancing equity out of other projects you own, selling other properties you own, or
bringing in a third party as an equity partner. If you provide the entire $5 million of anticipated
equity requirements, it will represent 75% of your net worth. You anticipate that you would receive a
$1 million development fee upon successful completion (included in the $25 million total cost).
Your research indicates that market rents are currently roughly $16 per square foot triple net, and
that development is occurring at a rate roughly commensurate with employment growth. Vacancy
rates are roughly 5% in the market and expected to remain stable. Cap rates for “comparable”
property sales range from 8-10%.
It is decision time. Would you accept their offer?

275

DR.

SUPPLEMENTAL VI

PI T E R L I N N E M A N

FINAL EXAM
React: (7 points) It is financially better for corporate users of real estate to rent their facilities than to own
them.

React: (7 points) The role of a special servicer for a CMBS issue is to keep track of which investment
tranche is the most special.

React: (7 points) Real estate opportunity funds are smarter than the typical investor, because they realize
higher equity IRRs than typical investors.

React: (7 points) REITs are the best type of real estate investments around, because they are publicly traded
companies.

React: (7 points) Mezzanine real estate financings always involve the lender taking a first security collateral
position in the property to which they are lending.

React: (7 points) The two most critical aspects of a lease for a shopping mall are the annual base rent and
the pass-through of CAM charges.

React: (7 points) The financial pro forma for a property represents the financial performance which will be
realized by the property, and as such reflects the risks of the investment.

React: (7 points) A building leased to a high credit quality tenant is more valuable than the same building
leased to a junk credit tenant.

React: (7 points) The due diligence process for a property is a legal exercise, and is not of much concern to
the businesspeople making the investment.

React: (47 points) Welcome to the Big Leagues

SUPPLEMENTAL VI

MID TERM EXAM
React: (5 Points) An unleased property is worth less than if the same property were fully leased.
React: (5 Points) A loan with an 80% loan-to-value ratio is preferable to a loan with a 50% loan-to-value
ratio.

React: (5 Points) When evaluating the attractiveness of an investment, you should focus mainly on your
equity IRR, because it tells you the return you will realize on your invested money.

React: (5 Points) When calculating property level cash flows, you should utilize earnings before interest
payments, because ongoing capital expenditures are roughly equal to depreciation allowances.

React: (21 Points) Wal-Mart has approached you with the opportunity to develop a 1 million square-foot
warehouse for them. In typical Wal-Mart fashion, they have given you a “take it or leave it” final
offer. The key terms are:
Facility Size

1,000,000 square feet, with 26,000,000 usable cubic feet

Lease Term

6 years

Rent

$7 per square foot

Operating Expenses

Wal-Mart pays all operating expenses, utilities, and property taxes

Renewal Options

7-year extension at $8 per square foot’ a second 7-year extension at
$9.50 per square foot; a third 7-year extension at $11.50 per square
foot, all at Wal-Mart’s option. Wal-Mart pays all operating
expenses, utilities, property taxes, and capital expenditures
undertaken during all lease renewal periods.

Penalties

If the property has not received its Certificate of
Occupancy within nine months, rent falls to $5 per square foot
during the first four years of the lease. If it is not received within
12 months, Wal-Mart is released from all obligations and receives a
$3 million penalty payment from the developer.

Property Specifications

Must meet all design and quality standards associated with a topquality bulk distribution facility.

You know the market and feel that the selected location is good, and that the warehouse market is in
good supply/demand balance. Vacancy rates are 4-5% in quality facilities, while net rents in the
market are running about $7.50-$8.00 per square foot.
You estimate that your development costs to complete this project (and hence receive your
Certificate of Occupancy) within 8 months are:
Hard Costs

$40 million

Your Development Fee

2 million

Interest Costs

3 million

Land

$16 million

Other Soft Costs

$ 9 million

Total

$70 million

You have a $54 million construction loan lined up, with a 1-year term for a 7% interest rate. You
believe that upon completion, you can sell the property for a 9% cap rate, with Wal-Mart credit as
the tenant. Alternatively, you believe that you could get a 10-year loan with 30-year amortization

You have a construction bid for the $40 million hard costs from a mid-sized local contractor that

experience indicates that it should take 30-33 weeks to complete the project and receive your
Certificate of Occupancy.
It’s decision time. Do you accept their bid or walk away? More importantly, why?
